    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A chromosome-scale genome assembly of Isatis indigotica , an important medicinal plant used in traditional Chinese medicine"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-02-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Isatis indigotica (2n&#8201;=&#8201;14) is an important medicinal plant in China. Its dried leaves and roots (called Isatidis Folium and Isatidis Radix, respectively) are broadly used in traditional Chinese medicine for curing diseases caused by bacteria and viruses such as influenza and viral pneumonia. Various classes of compounds isolated from this species have been identified as effective ingredients. Previous studies based on transcriptomes revealed only a few candidate genes for the biosynthesis of these active compounds in this medicinal plant. Here, we report a high-quality chromosome-scale genome assembly of I. indigotica with a total size of 293.88&#8201;Mb and scaffold N50&#8201;=&#8201;36.16&#8201;Mb using single-molecule real-time long reads and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture techniques. We annotated 30,323 high-confidence protein-coding genes. Based on homolog searching and functional annotations, we identified many candidate genes involved in the biosynthesis of main active components such as indoles, terpenoids, and phenylpropanoids. In addition, we found that some key enzyme-coding gene families related to the biosynthesis of these components were expanded due to tandem duplications, which likely drove the production of these major active compounds and explained why I. indigotica has excellent antibacterial and antiviral activities. Our results highlighted the importance of genome sequencing in identifying candidate genes for metabolite synthesis in medicinal plants."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Isatis indigotica (2n&#8201;=&#8201;14) is an important medicinal plant in China. Its dried leaves and roots (called Isatidis Folium and Isatidis Radix, respectively) are broadly used in traditional Chinese medicine for curing diseases caused by bacteria and viruses such as influenza and viral pneumonia. Various classes of compounds isolated from this species have been identified as effective ingredients. Previous studies based on transcriptomes revealed only a few candidate genes for the biosynthesis of these active compounds in this medicinal plant. Here, we report a high-quality chromosome-scale genome assembly of I. indigotica with a total size of 293.88&#8201;Mb and scaffold N50&#8201;=&#8201;36.16&#8201;Mb using single-molecule real-time long reads and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture techniques. We annotated 30,323 high-confidence protein-coding genes. Based on homolog searching and functional annotations, we identified many candidate genes involved in the biosynthesis of main active components such as indoles, terpenoids, and phenylpropanoids. In addition, we found that some key enzyme-coding gene families related to the biosynthesis of these components were expanded due to tandem duplications, which likely drove the production of these major active compounds and explained why I. indigotica has excellent antibacterial and antiviral activities. Our results highlighted the importance of genome sequencing in identifying candidate genes for metabolite synthesis in medicinal plants."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Isatis indigotica</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â14) is an important medicinal plant in China. Its dried leaves and roots (called Isatidis Folium and Isatidis Radix, respectively) are broadly used in traditional Chinese medicine for curing diseases caused by bacteria and viruses such as influenza and viral pneumonia. Various classes of compounds isolated from this species have been identified as effective ingredients. Previous studies based on transcriptomes revealed only a few candidate genes for the biosynthesis of these active compounds in this medicinal plant. Here, we report a high-quality chromosome-scale genome assembly of <i>I</i>. <i>indigotica</i> with a total size of 293.88âMb and scaffold N50â=â36.16âMb using single-molecule real-time long reads and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture techniques. We annotated 30,323 high-confidence protein-coding genes. Based on homolog searching and functional annotations, we identified many candidate genes involved in the biosynthesis of main active components such as indoles, terpenoids, and phenylpropanoids. In addition, we found that some key enzyme-coding gene families related to the biosynthesis of these components were expanded due to tandem duplications, which likely drove the production of these major active compounds and explained why <i>I. indigotica</i> has excellent antibacterial and antiviral activities. Our results highlighted the importance of genome sequencing in identifying candidate genes for metabolite synthesis in medicinal plants.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The plant family Brassicaceae (Cruciferae) comprises over 330 genera and ~3700 species with a worldwide distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rollins, R. C. The Cruciferae of Continental North America: Systematics of the Mustard Family from the Arctic to Panama (Stanford University Press, 1993)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d40061e481">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Al-Shehbaz, I. A. &amp; Mummenhoff, K. Transfer of the South African genera Brachycarpaea, Cycloptychis, Schlechteria, Silicularia, and Thlaspeocarpa to Heliophila (Brassicaceae). Novon 15, 385â389 (2005)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d40061e481_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Al-Shehbaz, I., Beilstein, M. A. &amp; Kellogg, E. Systematics and phylogeny of the Brassicaceae (Cruciferae): an overview. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 89â120 (2006)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d40061e481_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lysak, M. A. &amp; Koch, M. A. Phylogeny, genome, and karyotype evolution of crucifers (Brassicaceae). In Schmidt, R. &amp; Bancroft, I. (eds) Genetics and Genomics of the Brassicaceae 1â31 (Springer, New York, New York, USA, 2011)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40061e481_3">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Warwick, S. I., Francis, A. &amp; Al-Shehbaz, I. A. Brassicaceae: species checklist and database on CD-Rom. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 249â258 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d40061e484">5</a></sup>. Numerous crops are derived from this family, including vegetables (<i>Brassica</i> and <i>Raphanu</i>s), ornamentals (<i>Matthiola</i>, <i>Hesperis</i>, and <i>Lobularia</i>), spices (<i>Eutrema</i> and <i>Armoracia</i>), and medicines (<i>Isatis</i>). Based on sequenced genomes, several model species have been developed for diverse studies, including <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> for molecular function studies, <i>Brassica</i> for polyploidization and whole-genome duplication (WGD) studies, and <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i> for abiotic tolerance-related studies. However, genetic biosynthesis of the major active compounds in medicinal plants of this family remains poorly investigated.</p><p><i>Isatis indigotica</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â14) belongs to tribe Isatideae in lineage II of the family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Al-Shehbaz, I., Beilstein, M. A. &amp; Kellogg, E. Systematics and phylogeny of the Brassicaceae (Cruciferae): an overview. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 89â120 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d40061e531">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Beilstein, M., Al-Shehbaz, I. &amp; Kellogg, E. Brassicaceae phylogeny and trichome evolution. Am. J. Bot. 93, 607â619 (2006)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d40061e534">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Beilstein, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Sarah, M. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Brassicaceae phylogeny inferred from phytochrome A and ndhF sequence data: tribes and trichomes revisited. Am. J. Bot. 95, 1307â1327 (2008)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d40061e534_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Franzke, A., Lysak, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Koch, M. A. &amp; Mummenhoff, K. Cabbage family affairs: the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 108â116 (2011)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d40061e534_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guo, X. et al. Plastome phylogeny and early diversification of Brassicaceae. BMC Genomics 18, 176 (2017)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d40061e534_3">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nikolov, L. A. et al. Resolving the backbone of the Brassicaceae phylogeny for investigating trait diversity. N. Phytol. 222, 1638â1651 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d40061e537">10</a></sup>. This species is widely cultivated in China as an important medicinal plant because its dried leaves and roots are used as a traditional Chinese medicine for curing diseases and viruses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chang, S. J., Chang, Y. C., Lu, K. Z., Tsou, Y. Y. &amp; Lin, C. W. Antiviral activity of Isatis indigotica extract and its derived indirubin against Japanese encephalitis virus. Evid. Based Complement Alternat. Med. 2012, 925830 (2012)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d40061e541">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, S. et al. Antiviral action of Radix Isatidis and Folium Isatidis from different germplasm against influenza A virus. Acad. J. Second Mil. Med. Univ. 21, 204â206 (2000)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d40061e541_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Du, Z., Liu, H., Zhang, Z. &amp; Li, P. Antioxidant and anti-inflammatory activities of Radix Isatidis polysaccharide in murine alveolar macrophages. Int. J. Biol. Macromol. 58, 329â335 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d40061e544">13</a></sup>. The major active compounds isolated from this species comprise terpenoids, lignans, and indole alkaloids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, M. et al. Alkaloids from the root of Isatis indigotica. J. Nat. Prod. 75, 1167â1176 (2012)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40061e548">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Deng, X., Gao, G., Zheng, S. &amp; Li, F. Qualitative and quantitative analysis of flavonoids in the leaves of Isatis indigatica Fort. by ultra-performance liquid chromatography with PDA and electrospray ionization tandem mass spectrometry detection. J. Pharm. Biomed. 48, 562â567 (2008)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d40061e548_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, B. et al. Phenylpropanoids isolated from tetraploid roots of Isatis indigotica. Chin. Tradit. Herbal Drugs 36, 326â328 (2005)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40061e548_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhou, W. &amp; Zhang, X. Y. Research progress of Chinese herbal medicine Radix isatidis (banlangen). Am. J. Chin. Med. 41, 743â764 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d40061e551">17</a></sup>. These compounds were confirmed to have antiviral<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lin, C. W. et al. Anti-SARS coronavirus 3C-like protease effects of Isatis indigotica root and plant-derived phenolic compounds. Antivir. Res. 68, 36â42 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d40061e555">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Sun, D. D., Dong, W. W., Li, X. &amp; Zhang, H. Q. Indole alkaloids from the roots of Isatis ingigotica and their antiherpes simplex virus type 2 (HSV-2) activity in vitro. Chem. Nat. Compd. 46, 763â766 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d40061e558">19</a></sup>, antibacterial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Xia, X., Xiao, J., Shi, G. &amp; W., D. Function research of resistance to Salmonella Typhimurium infection using Banlangen polysaccharide. Med. J. Wuhan Univ. 28, 348â350 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d40061e563">20</a></sup>, anti-inflammatory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ho, Y. L. &amp; Chang, Y. S. Studies on the antinociceptive, anti-inflammatory and antipyretic effects of Isatis indigotica root. Among plant lignans, pinoresinol has the strongest antiinflammatory properties in human intestinal Caco-2 cells. J. Nutr. 142, 1798â1805 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d40061e570">22</a></sup>, and antileukemia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Hoessel, R. et al. Indirubin, the active constituent of a Chinese antileukaemia medicine, inhibits cyclin-dependent kinases. Nat. Cell Biol. 1, 60â67 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d40061e574">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Molina, P. et al. Inhibition of leukocyte functions by the alkaloid isaindigotone from Isatis indigotica and some new synthetic derivatives. J. Nat. Prod. 64, 1297â1300 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d40061e577">24</a></sup> functions. Previous studies based on transcriptomes revealed a few candidate genes involved in the biosynthesis of active compounds in this species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Biosynthesis of the active compounds of Isatis indigotica based on transcriptome sequencing and metabolites profiling. BMC Genomics 14, 857â857 (2013)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d40061e581">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gai, Q. Y. et al. Elicitation of Isatis tinctoria L. hairy root cultures by salicylic acid and methyl jasmonate for the enhanced production of pharmacologically active alkaloids and flavonoids. Plant Cell 137, 77â86 (2019)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d40061e581_1">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lu, B. B. et al. Cloning and characterization of a differentially expressed phenylalanine ammonialyase gene (IiPAL) after genome duplication from tetraploid Isatis indigotica Fort. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 48, 1439â1449 (2006)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d40061e581_2">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Hu, Y. et al. Isolation and characterization of a gene encoding cinnamoyl-CoA reductase from Isatis indigotica Fort. Mol. Biol. Rep. 38, 2075â2083 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d40061e584">28</a></sup>. However, the limitations of transcriptome quality and integrity hinder the identification of all candidate biosynthesis-related genes.</p><p>In the present study, we used single-molecule sequencing combined with high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) technology to assemble the genome and construct the pseudochromosomes of <i>I. indigotica</i>. Based on homolog searching and functional annotations, we aimed to identify candidate gene sets involved in the biosynthesis of putative active components. The candidate genes and genomic resources recovered here will be critically important for further experimental verification and artificial syntheses of the active compounds of this medicinal plant in the future.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and construction of pseudochromosomes</h3><p>The genome size, genome repeat size, and heterozygosity rate of <i>I. indigotica</i> were estimated using K-mer analysis. The 19-mer frequency of Illumina short reads with the highest peak occurred at a depth of 94. The genome was estimated to be 279.90âMb in size with 48.99% repeats, and the heterozygosity rate was estimated to be 0.44% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S3</a>). In addition, the genome size of <i>I. indigotica</i> was estimated to be ~305âMb based on flow cytometric analyses using <i>Vigna radiata</i> as the internal standard (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S2</a>).</p><p>We sequenced and assembled the genome of <i>I. indigotica</i> using single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technology from Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) and anchored the assembled contigs to seven pseudochromosomes using Hi-C techniques. The final chromosome-scale genome was 293.88âMb in length with 1199 contigs (contig N50â=â1.18âMb), a scaffold N50â=â36.17âMb, and a maximum pseudochromosome length of 38.25âMb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S4</a>, and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the final genome assembly of <i>I. indigotica</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The completeness of the genome assembly was evaluated using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="SimÃ£O, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Panagiotis, I., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d40061e718">29</a></sup>. Of the 1440 plant-specific orthologs, 1416 (98.33%) were identified in the assembly, of which 1400 (97.22%) were considered to be complete (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S5</a>). The assembly base accuracy was also assessed based on Illumina short read mapping. In total, 99.97% of the clean reads were mapped to the genome assembly, and 94.55% of them were properly mapped (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S6</a>). The base error percentage of the genome assembly was estimated to be 0.000081% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S7</a>). All these evaluations indicate the high completeness, high continuity, and high base accuracy of the present genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat and gene annotations</h3><p>Repetitive sequences were identified using a combination of ab initio and homology-based approaches. In total, we identified 53.27% of the assembled sequences as repetitive sequences, including 34.67% retrotransposons and 7.37% DNA transposons. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were found to account for 30.09% of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S8</a>). We annotated protein-coding genes by combining transcriptome-based, homology-based, and ab initio predictions. Finally, we predicted a total of 30,323 genes, of which 5973 had alternatively spliced transcripts. The average transcript length and coding sequence size were 2693 and 1387âbp, respectively, with a mean of 5.50 exons and 1.39 transcripts per gene (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Tab2">2</a>). Overall, 29,522 genes (97.36%) were assigned functions, and 76.16% and 91.69% of these genes had homologies and annotated proteins in the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases. Further functional annotations using InterProScan estimated that 95.86% of the genes contained conserved protein domains, and 87.32% of the genes were classified by Gene Ontology (GO) terms, with 29.41% mapped to known plant biological pathways based on the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathway database (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of predicted protein-coding genes in the <i>I. indigotica</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Chromosome structure of <i>I. indigotica</i>
</h3><p>Evolution of chromosome structures in Brassicaceae has been traced and established through comparative chromosome painting techniques using BAC probes of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lysak, M. A. &amp; Koch, M. A. Phylogeny, genome, and karyotype evolution of crucifers (Brassicaceae). In Schmidt, R. &amp; Bancroft, I. (eds) Genetics and Genomics of the Brassicaceae 1â31 (Springer, New York, New York, USA, 2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d40061e819">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="MandÃ¡kovÃ¡, T. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Painting of Arabidopsis chromosomes with chromosome-specific BAC clones. Curr. Protocols Plant Biol. 1, 359â371 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d40061e822">30</a></sup>. Using these techniques, Lysak and his colleagues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABCâs of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535â542 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d40061e826">31</a></sup> proposed a model comprising eight chromosomes and 24 genomic blocks (GBs, named from A to X) for comparative genomics and chromosomal analyses based on the Ancestral Crucifer Karyotype (ACK; <i>n</i>â=â8) concept<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schranz, M. E., Lysak, M. A. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. The ABCâs of comparative genomics in the Brassicaceae: building blocks of crucifer genomes. Trends Plant Sci. 11, 535â542 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d40061e833">31</a></sup>. This model was further updated to comprise 22 GBs by merging the four GBs to form two new blocks (K-L and M-N), and the GB boundaries defined by <i>A. thaliana</i> gene loci were also updated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lysak, M. A., MandÃ¡kovÃ¡, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 108â115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d40061e841">32</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig1">1a</a>). Six tribes (Calepineae, Coluteocarpeae, Conringieae, Eutremeae, Isatideae, and Sisymbrieae) of expanded lineage II were found to derive from a common ancestor with the Proto-Calepineae Karyotype (PCK; <i>n</i>â=â7). Among these tribes, three (Eutremeae, Isatideae, and Sisymbrieae) displayed an additional whole-arm translocation in the second and seventh chromosomes (translocation PCK, tPCK; <i>n</i>â=â7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lysak, M. A., MandÃ¡kovÃ¡, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 108â115 (2016)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d40061e854">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Terezie, M. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Chromosomal phylogeny and karyotype evolution in xâ=â7 crucifer species (Brassicaceae). Plant Cell 20, 2559â2570 (2008)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d40061e854_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541â1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d40061e857">34</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig1">1a</a>). ACK and PCK shared five similar chromosomes. Thus, they might descend from a common ancestor; alternatively, PCK may have evolved from ACK.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Ancestral Brassicaceae genomes and the distribution of ancestral GBs along the seven pseudochromosomes of I. indigotica."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Ancestral Brassicaceae genomes and the distribution of ancestral GBs along the seven pseudochromosomes of <i>I. indigotica</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="648" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> The ancestral genomes ACK, PCK, and tPCK, each comprising 22 ancestral GBs. Blocks with opposite orientations relative to that of ACK are represented by downward-pointing arrows. M-N and D GBs are translocated in tPCK chromosomes tPC2 and tPC7 compared with PCK chromosomes PC2 and PC7. The structures were drawn based on previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lysak, M. A., MandÃ¡kovÃ¡, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 108â115 (2016)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d40061e878">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Terezie, M. &amp; Lysak, M. A. Chromosomal phylogeny and karyotype evolution in xâ=â7 crucifer species (Brassicaceae). Plant Cell 20, 2559â2570 (2008)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d40061e878_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541â1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d40061e881">34</a></sup>. <b>b</b> Twenty-two GBs and their positions within the <i>I. indigotica</i> genome. Genes of each GB boundary are shown beside the chromosomes with the corresponding <i>A. thaliana</i> locus IDs within parentheses based on MCScanX results. The <i>A. thaliana</i> GB boundaries were derived from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541â1554 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d40061e898">34</a></sup></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine whether the <i>I. indigotica</i> genome sequence also supported tPCK structure in Isatideae, we compared the seven pseudochromosomes of <i>I. indigotica</i> with the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome by LAST and MCScanX. We determined syntenic relationships and constructed the order and orientation of the 22 GBs along the seven pseudochromosomes of the <i>I. indigotica</i> genome (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S6</a>). Based on the gene intervals of each GB of <i>A. thaliana</i>, we determined the corresponding intervals and boundaries of each block in <i>I. indigotica</i> and renamed the pseudochromosomes based on Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig1">1a</a> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S10</a>). The <i>I. indigotica</i> genome has good collinearity in each GB compared with the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome and is consistent with tPCK structure in both order and orientation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig1">1</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S6</a>). Furthermore, we carried out sequence alignments between the genomes of <i>I. indigotica</i> and the other three species that might also display tPCK structure (<i>Sisymbrium irio</i> for Sisymbrieae, <i>E. salsugineum</i> for Eutremeae, and <i>Schrenkiella parvula</i> for unassigned genera) using LAST. Our analyses suggested that these four species have similar chromosome structures (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S7</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S9</a>). However, we found obvious inversions in the <i>S. parvula</i> genome and low continuity of sequences in the <i>E. salsugineum</i> and <i>S. irio</i> genomes. These comparisons suggest that the present <i>I. indigotica</i> genome was better assembled in terms of both accuracy and continuity than others with tPCK structure.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Phylogenetic relationships and WGD analyses</h3><p>We clustered the annotated genes into gene families among <i>I. indigotica</i> and eight other Brassicaceae species with <i>Cleome hassleriana</i> as the outgroup. A total of 24,382 <i>I. indigotica</i> genes (80.41%) clustered into 18,900 gene families, of which 10,826 (57.28%) gene families were shared with nine other species and 896 (4.74%) were <i>I. indigotica</i> specific (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S11</a>). We selected 822 single-copy gene families among 10 species to construct a phylogenetic tree, which showed that <i>I. indigotica</i> was sister to <i>S. irio</i>. We further estimated the divergence time between them as 15.86 (12.71â19.20) million years ago (Mya) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig2">2a</a>). The relationships of all 10 species are consistent with those from previous phylogenetic analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Al-Shehbaz, I., Beilstein, M. A. &amp; Kellogg, E. Systematics and phylogeny of the Brassicaceae (Cruciferae): an overview. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 89â120 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1024">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Beilstein, M., Al-Shehbaz, I. &amp; Kellogg, E. Brassicaceae phylogeny and trichome evolution. Am. J. Bot. 93, 607â619 (2006)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1027">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Beilstein, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Sarah, M. &amp; Kellogg, E. A. Brassicaceae phylogeny inferred from phytochrome A and ndhF sequence data: tribes and trichomes revisited. Am. J. Bot. 95, 1307â1327 (2008)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1027_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Franzke, A., Lysak, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Koch, M. A. &amp; Mummenhoff, K. Cabbage family affairs: the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 108â116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1030">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Nikolov, L. A. et al. Resolving the backbone of the Brassicaceae phylogeny for investigating trait diversity. N. Phytol. 222, 1638â1651 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1033">10</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Evolutionary and comparative genomic analyses."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Evolutionary and comparative genomic analyses.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="696" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The phylogenetic tree of <i>I. indigotica</i> and eight other Brassicaceae species with <i>C. hassleriana</i> as the outgroup. All branch bootstrap values are 100. Gene family expansions are indicated in purple, while gene family contractions are indicated in light brown. The estimated divergence times (million years ago, Mya) are indicated at each node; bars are the 95% highest probability densities (HPDs). Circles in blue represent recent whole-genome duplication events. <b>b</b> Ks value distributions between <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>I. indigotica</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i>. <b>c</b> Orthogroups shared by selected species</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Then, we used synonymous substitution rates (Ks) between collinear paralogous genes to identify potential WGD events, based on the assumption that the number of silent substitutions per site between two homologous sequences increases in a relatively linear manner with time. A density plot of Ks values for the collinear gene pairs suggested that <i>I. indigotica</i> experienced a recent WGD event with a peak value of ~0.76, consistent with At-Î±-WGD for all Brassicaceae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Franzke, A., Lysak, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Koch, M. A. &amp; Mummenhoff, K. Cabbage family affairs: the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 108â116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1076">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bowers, J. E., Chapman, B. A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1079">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Barker, M. S., Heiko, V. &amp; Eric, S. M. Paleopolyploidy in the Brassicales: analyses of the cleome transcriptome elucidate the history of genome duplications in Arabidopsis and other Brassicales. Genome Biol. Evol. 1, 391â399 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1082">36</a></sup>. An independent WGD event was identified for <i>B. rapa</i> after its divergence from <i>I. indigotica</i> at Ksâ=â0.30â0.34, previously reported as a Brassiceae-specific triplication (Br-Î±-WGD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Franzke, A., Lysak, M. A., Al-Shehbaz, I. A., Koch, M. A. &amp; Mummenhoff, K. Cabbage family affairs: the evolutionary history of Brassicaceae. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 108â116 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1092">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lagercrantz, U. &amp; Lydiate, D. J. Comparative genome mapping in Brassica. Genetics 144, 1903â1910 (1996)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1095">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schranz, M. E., Mohammadin, S. &amp; Edger, P. P. Ancient whole genome duplications, novelty and diversification: the WGD Radiation Lag-Time Model. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 15, 147â153 (2012)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1095_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Tank, D. C. et al. Nested radiations and the pulse of angiosperm diversification: increased diversification rates often follow whole genome duplications. N. Phytol. 207, 454â467 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1098">39</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig2">2b</a>). Whole-genome alignment among the <i>I. indigotica</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>B. rapa</i> genomes carried out by LAST also confirmed the collinear relationship and these WGD events. For each genomic region of <i>I. indigotica</i>, we typically found one matching region in <i>A. thaliana</i> and three matching regions in <i>B. rapa</i>. These comparisons suggest that <i>I. indigotica</i> did not experience an independent WGD event after At-Î±-WGD (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S10</a>).</p><p>The expansion and contraction of gene families play critical roles in driving phenotypic diversification and enhancing special traits in plants. We discovered 1357 expanded and 3074 contracted gene families in <i>I. indigotica</i> relative to <i>S. irio</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig2">2a</a>). Tandem duplication was the main contributor to the gene family expansions. GO enrichment analysis of tandem repeat genes suggested that they were enriched in defense response to virus, indole biosynthetic process, lignin biosynthetic process, flavone synthase activity, and glucosyltransferase activity, some of which might be involved in the biosynthesis of active compounds in <i>I. indigotica</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S12</a>). We also performed GO enrichment analysis of the contracted gene families, and the results showed that they were enriched in proton export across plasma membrane, proton-exporting ATPase activity, regulation of stomatal movement, and defense response to other organism (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S13</a>), which are probably related to the environmental adaptation of the species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Identification of genes involved in the biosynthetic pathways of active compounds</h3><p>Based on the KEGG database, GO classification, and the suggested biosynthesis pathways, we used a combined method of homolog searching and functional annotation to identify candidate genes for the biosynthesis of three types of active compounds, namely, terpenoids, phenylpropanoids, and indoles, in <i>I. indigotica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, M. et al. Alkaloids from the root of Isatis indigotica. J. Nat. Prod. 75, 1167â1176 (2012)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1167">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Deng, X., Gao, G., Zheng, S. &amp; Li, F. Qualitative and quantitative analysis of flavonoids in the leaves of Isatis indigatica Fort. by ultra-performance liquid chromatography with PDA and electrospray ionization tandem mass spectrometry detection. J. Pharm. Biomed. 48, 562â567 (2008)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1167_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Li, B. et al. Phenylpropanoids isolated from tetraploid roots of Isatis indigotica. Chin. Tradit. Herbal Drugs 36, 326â328 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1170">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Chen, J. et al. Biosynthesis of the active compounds of Isatis indigotica based on transcriptome sequencing and metabolites profiling. BMC Genomics 14, 857â857 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1173">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Mohn, T., Plitzko, I. &amp; Hamburger, M. A comprehensive metabolite profiling of Isatis tinctoria leaf extracts. Phytochemistry 70, 924â934 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1176">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yang, L. et al. Indole alkaloids from the roots of Isatis indigotica and their inhibitory effects on nitric oxide production. Fitoterapia 95, 175â181 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1179">41</a></sup>. Sterols are the major terpenoids in <i>I. indigotica</i>, mainly comprising Î²-sitosterol and daucosterol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="He, L. W., Li, X. &amp; Chen, J. W. Research progress of antiviral active components of Radix Isatidis. Inform. Trad. Chin. Med. 22, 37â40 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1186">42</a></sup>. Î²-Sitosterol was reported to play a critical role in curing lung inflammation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yuk, J. E. et al. Effects of lactose-Î²-sitosterol and Î²-sitosterol on ovalbumin-induced lung inflammation in actively sensitized mice. Int. Immunopharmacol. 7, 1517â1527 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1190">43</a></sup>, while daucosterol can inhibit cancer cell proliferation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhao, C. et al. Daucosterol inhibits cancer cell proliferation by inducing autophagy through reactive oxygen species-dependent manner. Life Sci. 137, 37â43 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1194">44</a></sup>. A total of 59 genes in the present genome, which encoded 31 enzymes, were identified to be involved in terpenoid and sterol biosynthesis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S14</a>). Based on the functional annotations of these genes, the biosynthesis pathway of Î²-sitosterol is nearly complete and daucosterol can be further synthesized from Î²-sitosterol by glucosyltransferases (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig3">3a</a>). In addition, the intermediate product geranyl diphosphate can be used not only to synthesize sterols but also to produce secologanin for monoterpene indole alkaloids in numerous medicinal plants such as <i>Catharanthus roseus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kellner, F. et al. Genomeâguided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis. Plant J. 82, 680â692 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1207">45</a></sup>. However, we annotated genes only with geraniol 10-hydroxylase activity (GO: 0102811). The lack of other related genes may account for the absence of secologanin and other related monoterpene indole alkaloids in <i>I. indigotica</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Putative biosynthetic pathways of three main class active compounds in I. indigotica."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Putative biosynthetic pathways of three main class active compounds in <i>I. indigotica</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0240-5/MediaObjects/41438_2020_240_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="494" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The putative biosynthetic pathways of terpenoids (<b>a</b>), phenylpropanoids (<b>b</b>), and indole alkaloids (<b>c</b>) of active compounds in <i>I. indigotica</i>. Values within brackets indicate the numbers of gene copies corresponding to the catalytic genes in the pathways</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phenylpropanoids comprise lignans and flavonoids, with critical roles in anti-inflammatory response, anti-oxidant activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lv, H. et al. Isovitexin exerts anti-inflammatory and anti-oxidant activities on lipopolysaccharide-induced acute lung injury by inhibiting MAPK and NF-ÎºB and activating HO-1/Nrf2 pathways. Int. J. Biol. Sci. 12, 72â86 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1242">46</a></sup>, and attenuation of mammary tumor growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Saarinen, N. M. et al. Dietary lariciresinol attenuates mammary tumor growth and reduces blood vessel density in human MCF-7 breast cancer xenografts and carcinogen-induced mammary tumors in rats. Int. J. Cancer 123, 1196â1204 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1246">47</a></sup>. We annotated 66 genes involved in the biosynthesis of lignans and flavonoids (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#MOESM1">S15</a>). The identified putative pathway mainly comprises the biosynthesis of isovitexin and lariciresinol, while their glycosides were further synthesized by glucosyltransferases (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0240-5#Fig3">3b</a>). Indole alkaloids comprise another active component of <i>I. indigotica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Meng, L. J. et al. Diglycosidic indole alkaloid derivatives from an aqueous extract of Isatis indigotica roots. J. Asian Nat. Prod. Res. 19, 529 (2017)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1258">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, Y. et al. Fitoterapia 82, 288â292 (2011)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d40061e1258_1">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Liu, Y.
